ونحو فلق البحر، وإخراج يده بيضاء، وغير ذلك، وما أتى به من الجواد والقمل والضفادع والدم، وغير ذلك مما لا يقدر عليه البشر؟ فإن من ذلك وإنما كان يدعو ويرغب. قالوا: موسى لم يكن مخترعا الشيء إلى الله تعالى في أن يظهر على يديه ذلك، يقال لهم: فما أنكرتم أن تكون هذه حال عيسى أن يظهر الآيات على يده؟ وأنه كان يرغب إلى خالقه وربه ومالكه في ذلك. وقد نطق الإنجيل بذلك لأن في الإنجيل أن عيسى عليه السلام بكى فقال: «رب ! إن كان في مشيئتك أن تصرف هذه الكأس عن أحد فاصرفها عني، وأنه أراد أن يحيي كهلا فقال: يا أبي ! أدعوك كما كنت أدعوك فتستجيب لي، وإنما أدعوك من أجل هؤلاء ليعلموا، وقال : ( يا أبي ! أنا أحمدك، وقال وهو على الخشبة وقت الصلب بزعمهم : إلهي ! إلهي ! لم تركتني ؟»، وهذا وتضرعه وابتهاله فوجب أنه عبد مربوب ومحدث مخلوق كموسى وغيره من الرسل عليهم السلام. فإن قالوا: عيسى كان يدعو ويرغب بهذا الدعاء على سبيل التعليم للأتباع والتلاميذ؛ وإلا فقد كان يخترع الآيات اختراعاً ويأمر أن يكون فيكون، قيل لهم: فما أنكرتم من أن يكون دعاء موسى ورغبته إنما وقع على سبيل التعليم؟ وإلا فقد كان يخترع فلق البحر وإخراج اليد بيضاء وقلب العصا ثعباناً وتظليلهم بالغمام واختراع المن والسلوى ويأمر بأن يكون ذلك فيكون فلا يجدون إلى ذلك مدفعاً. فإن قالوا: قولنا «مسيح» اسم لمعنيين: لاهوت، هو إله، وناسوت، هو إنسان مخلوق؛ فما كان من تضرع ودعاء فإنما وقع من الإنسان الذي هو الناسوت؛ وما كان من إحداث آية وإظهار معجزة فهو واقع من الإله دون الإنسان يقال لهم: فما أنكرتم من أن يكون موسى أيضاً إسماً لمعنيين : إله وإنسان؛ فما كان من دعاء ورغبة فإنه واقع من الناسوت، وما كان من اختراع آية أو إبداع معجزة فإنه من اللاهوت دون الناسوت؛ ولا فصل في ذلك. فإن قالوا كل واحد من هؤلاء الأنبياء قد أقر بلسانه بأنه مخلوق وعبد مربوب مألوه مرسل من عند الله، عزّ وجلّ والمسيح لم يقر بذلك قيل لهم: وكذلك المسيح قد اعترف بأنه نبي مرسل وعبد مخلوق؛ لأن الإنجيل ينطق بأنه قال: «إني عبد الله وأرسلت معلماً»، وقال: «فكما بعثني أبي فكذلك أبعثكم عمدوا الناس وغسلوهم باسم الأب والابن والروح القدس قال في الإنجيل: إنّ النّبي لا يُكرم في مدينته»، في نظائر ذلك.
And such as the splitting of the sea, and the bringing forth of his hand white, and other things, and what he brought of the locusts and lice and frogs and blood, and other things which humans are not able to do? For from that, he was only supplicating and desiring. They said: Moses was not an inventor of the thing to Allah Almighty so that it appeared at his hand. It is said to them: What prevents you from this being the case of Jesus—that signs appear at his hand? And that he was desiring to his Creator and his Lord and his Owner in that. And the Gospel has stated that, because in the Gospel Jesus, peace be upon him, wept and said: 'Lord! If it is in Your will to turn this cup away from anyone, then turn it away from me.' And that he wanted to revive an old man and said: 'O my Father! I call upon You as I used to call upon You and You answer me, and I only call upon You for the sake of these people that they may know.' And he said: 'O my Father! I praise You.' And he said while on the wood at the time of the crucifixion according to their claim: 'My God! My God! Why have You forsaken me?' This and his supplication and his pleading necessitate that he is a servant, owned, created, originated like Moses and other messengers, peace be upon them. If they say: Jesus was supplicating and desiring with this supplication for the purpose of teaching his followers and disciples; otherwise he used to invent signs inventively and command and it would be. It is said to them: What prevents you from the supplication of Moses and his desire occurring only for the purpose of teaching? Otherwise he used to invent the splitting of the sea, the bringing forth of the hand white, the turning of the staff into a serpent, their shading with clouds, the invention of manna and quails, and he commands that it be and it is, and they find no way to refute that. If they say: Our statement 'Messiah' is a name for two meanings: Lahut (divine nature) which is God, and Nasut (human nature) which is a created human; so whatever was of supplication and prayer, it occurred from the human which is the Nasut; and whatever was of bringing about a sign and showing a miracle, it is from the Lahut, not from the human. It is said to them: What prevents you from Moses also being a name for two meanings: God and human? So whatever was of supplication and desire, it occurs from the Nasut, and whatever was of invention of a sign or creation of a miracle, it is from the Lahut, not from the Nasut; and there is no distinction in that. If they say: Every one of these prophets has acknowledged with his tongue that he is created and a servant, owned, worshipped, sent from Allah, Almighty and Majestic, and the Messiah did not acknowledge that. It is said to them: Likewise, the Messiah has acknowledged that he is a prophet sent and a created servant; because the Gospel states that he said: 'I am the servant of God and I have been sent as a teacher.' And he said: 'As my Father sent me, so I send you: baptize people and wash them in the name of the Father, the Son, and the Holy Spirit.' He said in the Gospel: 'A prophet is not honored in his own town.' And other similar instances.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of Christian objections regarding the divinity of Jesus. It consists of four arguments, each following an objection-answer format. The author, Imam Baqillani, uses the example of Moses to demonstrate the inconsistency in Christian reasoning. 

First objection: Christians argue that Moses did not invent miracles himself but only supplicated to God, implying that his miracles were not his own. Baqillani responds by asking why the same cannot be said for Jesus, citing Gospel passages where Jesus prays and supplicates, thus proving that Jesus also relied on God. This establishes Jesus as a servant and creature like Moses. 

Second objection: Christians claim Jesus' prayers were merely for teaching his followers, while he actually created miracles by his own command. Baqillani turns this back: Moses also commanded miracles (splitting the sea, etc.), so why cannot his prayers also be for teaching? The argument is symmetrical. 

Third objection: Christians propose a dual nature for Christ: a divine nature (Lahut) and a human nature (Nasut). All supplication and human acts come from the human nature, while miracles come from the divine nature. Baqillani retorts by suggesting that Moses could also be considered a name for two natures, with the same division, thus nullifying the Christian claim of uniqueness. 

Fourth objection: Christians assert that all prophets confessed their servitude except Jesus. Baqillani refutes this by quoting Gospel verses where Jesus calls himself a servant of God and a sent teacher, and other statements indicating his prophetic status. The argument systematically dismantles each objection by applying the same logic to Moses, showing that Christian reasoning is arbitrary.

Key Terms
عبد
رسول
إنجيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Christians believe Jesus never claimed to be a servant or prophet.
Correction: The Gospel records Jesus calling himself a servant of God and a prophet.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Baqillani provide that Jesus acknowledged being a servant?
A) Jesus said he came to abolish the law.
B) Jesus said 'I am the servant of God and I have been sent as a teacher.'
C) Jesus claimed to be God.
D) The disciples said Jesus was a servant.

Key Takeaway
Jesus' own words in the Gospel confirm he is a servant and prophet, not divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Jesus, a great prophet, openly declared himself a 'servant of God.' In what areas of your life do you struggle to acknowledge that you are entirely dependent on Allah? What specific act of humble submission can you perform today to mirror that confession?
?
When the Gospel records Jesus saying 'A prophet is not honored in his own town,' it warns against rejecting truth due to familiarity. Is there a truth about Allah or Islam that you have been resisting because it came from someone close to you? How will you open your heart to it?
?
If even Jesus needed to affirm his servitude, how much more do you? Write down one area where pride has prevented you from fully submitting to Allah. Commit to a concrete change this week.

Analogy of Moses Refutes Christ's Two Natures
Baqillani uses Moses to argue that Christ's two natures are not unique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the example of Moses to challenge the uniqueness of Christ's two natures.

Core Idea
If Moses can be said to have two natures, then Christ's case is not unique.

Baqillani engages with Christian theology by drawing a parallel between Christ and Moses. The Christian claim that Christ's two natures explain his miracles and prayers is refuted by showing that the same logic applies to Moses, who also performed miracles and made supplications. This passage demonstrates a key method of interfaith theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice how Baqillani structures his argument with a hypothetical objection and response.
The passage begins with a reference to Moses' miracles before introducing the Christian claim.
2
Identify the Christian claim
The Christians assert that 'Messiah' refers to two meanings: divine nature (lahut) and human nature (nasut). They attribute miracles to the divine nature and supplications to the human nature.
This is a classic two-natures doctrine.
3
Analyze Baqillani's counter-analogy
Baqillani asks why the same reasoning cannot apply to Moses. If Moses also performed miracles and prayed, then by the same logic, Moses would have two natures as well.
The analogy undermines the uniqueness of Christ's two natures.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that there is no distinction (la fasl) between the cases, thereby refuting the Christian argument.
The strength of the argument lies in logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون فيكون، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من أن يكون دعاء موسى ورغبته إنما وقع على سبيل التعليم؟ وإلا فقد كان يخترع فلق البحر وإخراج اليد بيضاء وقلب العصا ثعباناً وتظليلهم بالغمام واختراع المن والسلوى ويأمر بأن يكون ذلك فيكون فلا يجدون إلى ذلك مدفعاً . فإن قالوا : قولنا «مسيح» اسم لمعنيين: لاهوت، هو إله، وناسوت، هو إنسان مخلوق ؛ فما كان من تضرع ودعاء فإنما وقع من الإنسان الذي هو الناسوت ؛ وما كان من إحداث آية وإظهار معجزة فهو واقع من الإله دون الإنسان يقال لهم : فما أنكرتم من أن يكون موسى أيضاً إسماً لمعنيين : إله وإنسان؛ فما كان من دعاء ورغبة فإنه واقع من الناسوت، وما كان من اختراع آية أو إبداع معجزة فإنه من اللاهوت دون الناسوت؛ ولا فصل في ذلك.
He says 'Be and it is.' It was said to them: Why do you deny that Moses' supplication and request occurred only as a teaching? Otherwise, he used to create the splitting of the sea, the bringing forth of the white hand, the turning of the staff into a serpent, the shading of clouds, the creation of manna and quail, and he would command something to be and it would be, and they have no way to refute that. If they say: Our saying 'Messiah' is a name for two meanings: divinity (lahut), which is God, and humanity (nasut), which is a created human; whatever was of supplication and prayer occurred from the human who is the nasut; and whatever was of producing a sign or manifesting a miracle occurred from the divinity without the human. It is said to them: Why do you deny that Moses is also a name for two meanings: a divinity and a human? So whatever was of supplication and desire occurs from the nasut, and whatever was of invention of a sign or creation of a miracle occurs from the lahut without the nasut; and there is no distinction in that.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by analogy. The Christian claim is that Christ (al-Masih) has two distinct natures: a divine nature (lahut) responsible for miracles, and a human nature (nasut) responsible for mundane actions like supplication. Baqillani first notes that Moses also performed extraordinary miracles (splitting the sea, white hand, staff as serpent, etc.) and also made supplications. If the Christians insist on a dual-nature explanation for Christ, then logically the same could be said of Moses: his miracles would come from a divine nature and his prayers from a human nature. The author concludes that there is no distinction (faṣl) between the two cases, thus undermining the Christian argument's uniqueness.

لاهوت
ناسوت
إله
إنسان
آية
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two-natures doctrine is unique to Christ without any parallel in other prophets.
Correction: Baqillani shows that Moses also performed miracles and prayed, so the same reasoning would assign him two natures, removing uniqueness.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use to refute the Christian claim about Christ's two natures?
A) The miracles of Muhammad
B) The example of Moses
C) The testimony of the Gospels
D) Philosophical arguments about essence

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the uniqueness of Christ's two natures by showing that the same logic applies to Moses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a belief about yourself that you would never apply to others? Baqillani shows that Christians hold Christ to a standard they don't apply to Moses. Where in your life do you treat your own actions or status differently from how you judge others? What would it take to be fully consistent?
?
Baqillani's argument forces consistency between belief and application. Is there an inconsistency between what you claim to believe about Allah's power and how you rely on your own efforts? If someone applied your logic to another person, would it hold up? Examine your heart and commit to one area where you will align your practice more closely with your profession of tawhid.

Christ's Confessions in the Gospel Prove He Is Not God
Baqillani cites Gospel passages where Christ calls himself a servant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Gospel passages to argue that Christ confessed to being a servant and prophet, thus not God.

Core Idea
Christ's own words in the Gospel show he is a servant, not God.

Baqillani addresses a Christian claim that Christ never confessed to being a created servant like other prophets. He responds by directly quoting from the Gospels where Christ calls himself a servant and a prophet. This passage demonstrates the Islamic method of using the opponents' own scriptures to refute their claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage carefully, noticing how Baqillani sets up the Christian objection and his counterargument.
The objection claims Christ never confessed to being a servant.
2
Identify the Gospel quotes
Find the specific Gospel statements Baqillani cites and consider their implication.
There are three distinct quotes.
3
Analyze the conclusion
Understand why Baqillani concludes that Christ must not be divine.
The conclusion follows from the evidence of Christ's own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا كل واحد من هؤلاء الأنبياء قد أقر بلسانه بأنه مخلوق وعبد مربوب مألوه مرسل من عند الله، عزّ وجلّ والمسيح لم يقر بذلك قيل لهم : وكذلك المسيح قد اعترف بأنه نبي مرسل وعبد مخلوق؛ لأن الإنجيل ينطق بأنه قال: «إني عبد الله وأُرسلت معلماً»، وقال: «فكما بعثني أبي فكذلك أبعثكم عمدوا الناس وغسلوهم باسم الأب والابن والروح القدس قال في الإنجيل: إنّ النّبي لا يُكرم في مدينته»، في نظائر هذه الإقرارات عنه كثيرة بأنه نبي وعبد مرسل ومألوه مدبَّر، فوجب أنه ليس بإله.
If they say: Each one of these prophets has confessed with his tongue that he is a created being and a subjugated servant, sent from God, and the Messiah did not confess that. It is said to them: Likewise, the Messiah has acknowledged that he is a sent prophet and a created servant, because the Gospel states that he said: 'I am a servant of God and I was sent as a teacher,' and said: 'Just as my Father sent me, so I send you; baptize people and wash them in the name of the Father, the Son, and the Holy Spirit.' He said in the Gospel: 'A prophet is not honored in his own town.' Among similar confessions from him are many that he is a prophet and a sent servant and a subjugated being, so it is necessary that he is not God.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents a Christian objection and Baqillani's refutation. The Christians claim that all other prophets confessed to being created servants, but Christ never did. Baqillani responds by quoting from the Gospels where Christ says he is a servant of God, a teacher sent by God, and that a prophet is not honored in his own town. The author also references Christ commanding baptism in the name of the Father, Son, and Holy Spirit — a Trinitarian formula that Baqillani uses to show Christ's own confession of being sent. The conclusion (wajaba) is that Christ cannot be divine, since he repeatedly described himself as a prophet and servant.

Correction: Baqillani shows from the Gospel that Christ did confess to being a servant and a prophet.

D) By referring to Jewish scriptures

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses Christ's own words from the Gospel to prove he is a prophet and servant, not divine.

?

?

?

Applying the Nasut-Lahut Distinction Consistently
Baqillani turns the Christian objection back on itself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani exposes the inconsistency in the Christian use of the nasut-lahut distinction by universalizing its application to all prophets.

Core Idea
If the distinction excuses Christ, it excuses every prophet.

In the previous passage, Baqillani cited biblical confessions where Christ acknowledges being a servant and prophet. Christians now try to sidestep this by claiming those confessions come only from Christ's human nature (nasut), not his divine nature (lahut). Baqillani turns this objection back on them by asking why the same logic would not apply to every prophet. This lesson walks through that devastating counter-objection and its implications for Christian claims about Christ's uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Christian objection
The Christians argue that confessions of servitude and prophethood from Christ are from his human nature alone. Recognize this as an attempt to preserve Christ's divinity while dismissing the confessions.
This is a common theological dodge: attributing human actions to the human nature and divine actions to the divine nature.
2
Read Baqillani's counter-objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Baqillani does not directly reject the distinction but instead applies it universally to all prophets.
The rhetorical question 'Do you find any distinction?' is the key.
3
Analyze the logical inconsistency
If every prophet's confession of being a servant or prophet is attributed to their nasut, then Christ is not unique in this regard. The Christian special pleading collapses.
The burden is on Christians to show why Christ's case is different.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this argument demonstrates the necessity of consistency in theological reasoning. Baqillani uses the opponent's own premise against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا هذه الإقرارات واقعة من ناسوت المسيح دون لاهوته، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون كل إقرار سمع من نبي بأنه خلق وعبد ونبي فإنه إقرار ناسوته دون لاهوته ، فهل تجدون في ذلك فصلا؟
If they say: These confessions occurred from the nasut of the Messiah, not from his lahut. It is said to them: Why do you deny that every confession heard from a prophet that he is a created being, a servant, and a prophet is a confession of his nasut, not of his lahut? Do you find any distinction in that?
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a direct counter-objection. The Christians try to evade the previous refutation by claiming that Christ's confessions came only from his human nature (nasut), not from his divine nature (lahut). Baqillani retorts by universalizing the principle: if that is true for Christ, then it must also be true for every prophet. Every confession of being a servant or prophet uttered by any prophet would then be attributed only to their human nature. The author asks rhetorically whether the Christians can find any valid distinction (faṣl) between the cases, thereby exposing the inconsistency of their special pleading.

Key Terms
إقرار
ناسوت
لاهوت
نبي
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the human/divine distinction in Christ allows selective attribution of actions, so that Christ's confessions do not affect his divinity.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if such attribution is valid for Christ, it must apply to all prophets, thereby undermining Christ's claimed uniqueness.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani point out about the Christian argument that Christ's confessions are from his human nature?
A) It is unique to Christ
B) It would apply to all prophets
C) It contradicts the Gospels
D) It is a valid distinction

Key Takeaway
Consistency demands that if the nasut-lahut distinction excuses Christ's confessions, it must excuse those of all prophets, thereby eliminating any special status for Christ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Baqillani's argument, you see the logical force of consistency. Have you ever accepted a double standard in your own belief about God, allowing yourself to excuse something in one area that you condemn in another? Will you now examine your heart for such inconsistencies?
?
The Christians claimed a distinction to protect Christ's divinity. But what about you? Do you ever use mental distinctions to protect your own ego or sin, attributing your good deeds to your 'true self' and your sins to your 'human weakness'? Are you willing to be consistent before God?
?
Baqillani's challenge is meant to expose contradiction. When you face a contradiction between your actions and your professed beliefs, do you honestly ask yourself whether any distinction you make is legitimate, or do you just content yourself with a comfortable excuse? What will you do today to close that gap?

Scriptural Names for Christ Are Not Divine Declarations
Baqillani interprets the scriptural title 'God' for Christ as human exaggeration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the Christian proof text that Christ is called 'God' in scripture by analyzing the language and possibility of exaggeration.

Core Idea
Scriptural titles for great figures can be metaphorical or exaggerated, not necessarily literal divine declarations.

Baqillani engages with a key Christian argument: that scripture itself calls Christ 'God'. He responds by examining the exact wording and drawing a parallel with Moses, who was also called 'god' in a figurative sense. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows how careful attention to language and context can refute apparently strong proof texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the parallel with Moses.
2
Identify the Christian claim and initial response
Note the Christian proof text and Baqillani's initial response using the Moses parallel.
3
Analyze the wording 'yud'ā ilāhan'
Notice that scripture says 'he shall be called God', not 'he is God'. This opens the door to interpretation.
The Arabic verb form implies calling, not being.
4
Consider the possibility of exaggeration
Baqillani suggests people might exaggerate his veneration. Reflect on how this undermines the Christian certainty.
5
Conclude the argument
Baqillani concludes that Christians cannot prove literal divinity from this text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا إنما قلنا إن المسيح إله لأن الله قال في الكتب إنه إله وسمّاه بذلك فقال : (العذراء البتول تحمل وتلد ابناً يدعى إلهاً)، يقال لهم : فقد قلتم قال الله لموسى: «إني جعلتك إلهاً لهارون وجعلتك إلهاً لفرعون»، على معنى أنك مدبّر له وآمر له وواجب عليه طاعتك ؛ فقد كانت هذه لغة . ثم يقال لهم : لم يخبر الله تعالى بأنه هو سماه أو يسميه إلهاً ، وإنما قال : يدعى اسمه إلهاً، فيمكن أن يكون أراد أن قوماً يغلون في تعظيمه ويدعونه بذلك ويتجاوزون به الحد ويكذبون في ذلك ويفترون فمن أين لكم أن ما سمي به من ذلك واجب صحيح ؟ فلا يجدون إلى ذلك سبيلاً.
If they say: We only said that the Messiah is God because God said in the scriptures that he is God and named him as such, saying: 'The virgin virgin shall conceive and bear a son who shall be called God.' It is said to them: You have said that God said to Moses: 'I have made you a god to Aaron and made you a god to Pharaoh,' meaning that you are a director for him, a commander to him, and his obedience is obligatory upon you; so this was a language. Then it is said to them: God did not inform that He Himself named him or called him God; rather, He said: 'His name shall be called God.' So it is possible that He meant that a people would exaggerate in his veneration and call him by that, exceeding the limit and lying in that and slandering. So how do you know that what he is named with from that is necessarily correct? They have no way to that.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian proof text that Christ is called 'God' in scripture. The author first notes a parallel: God said to Moses, 'I have made you a god to Aaron and Pharaoh,' which is understood figuratively (as a director or authority). Then Baqillani analyzes the exact wording of the prophecy: 'He shall be called God' (yud'ā ilāhan) — not 'He is God' or 'God named him'. This could mean that people will call him God in exaggeration (ghuluww), exceeding proper bounds. The author questions how the Christians can be certain that such a title is necessarily true, given the possibility of metaphorical or exaggerated usage. The argument concludes that the Christians have no valid way to prove that the title is literally correct.

Key Terms
إله
الكتب
لغة
غلو
تعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That scriptural titles always reflect divine truth without possibility of figurative or exaggerated usage.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the phrase 'he shall be called God' can be interpreted as human exaggeration, not a literal divine declaration, and that a parallel exists with Moses being called 'god' in a figurative sense.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani interpret the phrase 'he shall be called God'?
A) As a literal declaration of divinity
B) As a possible reference to human exaggeration
C) As a command to worship Christ
D) As a translation error

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the scriptural phrase 'he shall be called God' does not prove Christ's divinity because it could refer to human exaggeration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read scripture or hear religious claims, do you ever assume a literal meaning without considering the possibility of metaphorical or exaggerated language? How might that lead you astray?
?
Reflect on a time when you attributed a title or status to someone beyond what was due. How does Baqillani’s warning about ghuluww (exaggeration) apply to your own tendency to elevate people?
?
If the Christians can be mistaken about the meaning of a scriptural title, what assurance do you have that your own interpretations of Islamic texts are correct? What safeguards do you have against misinterpretation?

Refuting the Analogy of Jesus' Birth
Baqillani refutes the Christian analogy of Jesus's birth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses logical consistency to refute the Christian claim that Jesus's miraculous birth proves his divinity.

Core Idea
If fatherless birth means divinity, then Adam, Eve, and angels are more divine.

This passage shows a powerful Islamic refutation of a Christian argument. The Christians claimed that Jesus's birth without a father indicates his divine nature. Baqillani turns this reasoning against them, showing that by the same logic, other beings without parents would be even more deserving of divinity. Let's examine how he constructs this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of Baqillani's argument.
Notice the pattern: each example is more extreme than the last.
2
Identify the Christian claim
Understand that the Christian claim being refuted is that Jesus's birth without a father makes him divine.
Baqillani is not attacking Christianity broadly, but a specific argument.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Trace how Baqillani applies the same logic to Adam (no parents), Eve (created from rib without mother), and angels (created without parents). Each is further removed from ordinary birth.
The argument shows the Christian claim leads to absurd conclusions.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why this refutation is effective: it accepts the premise but shows it proves too much.
This is a common Islamic theological method called ilzām (forcing a conclusion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا من فحل وليس كذلك من ذكرتموه من الرسل فيقال لهم: فيجب على قياسكم أن يكون آدم عليه السلام إلهاً لأنه وجد لا من ذكر ولا أنثى فهو أبعد عن صفة المحدث لأنه لم يحل بطن مريم ولا غيرها ولا كان من معدن ولد ولا موضع حمل. وكذلك يجب أن تكون حواء رباً لأنها خلقت من ضلع آدم من غير ذكر ولا أنثى فهو أبعد. وكذلك المطالبة عليهم في وجوب كون الملائكة آلهة لأنهم لا من ذكر ولا أنثى ولا على وجه التبني.
Not from a stallion, and likewise not from those you mentioned from the messengers, so it is said to them: Then it must be according to your analogy that Adam, peace be upon him, be a god because he came into existence not from a male nor a female, and he is farther removed from the attribute of originatedness because he did not enter the womb of Mary nor any other, nor was he from a mine of birth nor a place of gestation. Likewise Eve must be a lord because she was created from Adam's rib without a male nor a female, so she is farther removed. And similarly the objection is brought against them regarding the necessity of angels being gods because they are not from a male nor a female nor in the manner of adoption.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by analogy. Baqillani argues that if the Christian claim that Jesus is divine because his birth lacked a father is accepted, then by the same logic, Adam (who had neither father nor mother), Eve (created from Adam's rib), and angels (created without parents) would be more deserving of divinity. This exposes the inconsistency of the Christian argument.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās, analogy)
الأبعد (al-ab‘ad, more remote)
المحدث (al-muḥdath, originated thing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this is a simple comparison, but it is a logical reductio ad absurdum.
Correction: Baqillani is not merely comparing births; he is forcing an absurd conclusion from the Christians' own premise.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the Christian claim that Baqillani refutes here?
A) Jesus performed miracles
B) Jesus was born without a father
C) Jesus claimed to be God
D) Jesus rose from the dead

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the Christian argument for Jesus's divinity based on his fatherless birth is self-defeating, as it would imply that Adam, Eve, and angels are also divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you have never examined with logical consistency. What would Baqillani's method reveal if applied to it? Are you willing to apply that scrutiny today?
?
The Christians rejected Baqillani's argument because it challenged their core belief. When has your attachment to a belief made you resistant to a clear logical refutation? How can you cultivate humility in the face of truth?
?
Baqillani uses the absurdity of multiple gods to correct a flawed analogy. Are there any 'gods' in your life — pursuits, comforts, or identities — that you have elevated without realizing the logical inconsistency with your faith? What step will you take to dismantle that false deity?

Interpreting 'I and the Father Are One'
Baqillani interprets the Gospel saying 'I and the Father are one' metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational refutation of the literal interpretation of 'I and the Father are one' and his metaphorical interpretation as oneness in obedience.

Core Idea
'One' means in obedience, not in essence.

Imam Baqillani here addresses a Christian argument that Jesus's divinity is proven by his statement 'I and the Father are one.' Baqillani offers a metaphorical interpretation: unity in obedience, and then points out the absurd consequences of taking it literally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Baqillani's argument unfold.
Notice the structure: Christian claim, then response.
2
Identify the Christian claim
The Christian opponents argue that Jesus's lordship is established because he said 'I and the Father are one' in the Gospel.
This claim is the starting point of Baqillani's response.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
Baqillani interprets 'one' as oneness in obedience: whoever obeys Jesus obeys the Father. Also 'seeing' means seeing through hearing His commands.
This avoids attributing divine essence to Jesus.
4
Note the reductio ad absurdum
If taken literally, Jesus's human attributes (birth, eating, crucifixion) would apply to the Father, which is impossible for God.
This shows the literal reading is irrational.
5
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this argument defends Islamic monotheism (tawhid) while preserving respect for Jesus as a prophet.
Baqillani's method is typical of kalam: rational critique of scriptural claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما وجب القضاء على ربوبيته؛ لأنه قال في الإنجيل، وهو الصادق المصدوق في قوله : «أنا وأبي واحد ومن رآني فقد رأى أبي، يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون معنى ذلك أن من أطاعني فقد أطاع أبي أي مرسلي ومعلمي الحكمة؛ ومن عصاني فقد عصاه؛ فيكون معنى أبي أي إنه معلمي ومرسلي؛ وقوله : «فمن رآني فقد رآه معناه فكأنه قد رآه وسمع كلامه وأمره ونهيه؛ لأنه لو هو وأبوه واحداً لوجب أن تكون الولادة والحمل والقتل والصلب والأكل والشرب والحركة الجارية عليه كل ذلك جارياً على الأب وإذا كان هو المتحد بالجسد أن يكون الأب متحداً به، فهذا كله ترك لقولهم إن ركبوه.
If they say: The judgment of his lordship is necessarily established because he said in the Gospel, and he is truthful and believed in his saying: 'I and my Father are one, and whoever sees me has seen my Father', it is said to them: Why do you not deny that the meaning of that is that whoever obeys me has obeyed my Father, i.e., my sender and teacher of wisdom; and whoever disobeys me has disobeyed Him? So the meaning of 'my Father' is that He is my teacher and my sender. And His saying 'Whoever sees me has seen Him' means it is as if he has seen Him and heard His speech, His command and His prohibition. For if he and his Father were one, then it would be necessary that birth, pregnancy, killing, crucifixion, eating, drinking, and the movements occurring upon him all occur upon the Father. And if he is the one united with the body, then the Father must be united with it. All of this invalidates their saying if they adopt it.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage provides a metaphorical interpretation of the Gospel verses. Baqillani argues that 'one' means in obedience, not in essence. He also points out the absurd consequences of taking it literally: if Jesus and the Father are one in essence, then all human attributes of Jesus would apply to the Father, which is impossible for God.

Key Terms
الإنجيل (al-Injīl, Gospel)
الأب (al-Ab, Father)
الإلهية (al-ilāhiyya, divinity)
اللاهوت (al-lāhūt, divinity)
الناسوت (al-nāsūt, humanity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies Jesus's respect or prophethood.
Correction: Baqillani is not denying Jesus's status as a respected prophet; he is rationally refuting the Christian doctrine of divinity by showing the literal interpretation is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say the phrase 'I and my Father are one' means?
A) Oneness of essence
B) Oneness in obedience
C) Oneness in will
D) Oneness in nature

Key Takeaway
Baqillani interprets 'I and the Father are one' as oneness in obedience, not essence, and shows the literal reading leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse or hadith that seems to conflict with God's transcendence, are you willing to consider a metaphorical interpretation, or do you cling to a literal reading even if it leads to absurdity? How does Baqillani's method challenge your approach?
?
If obeying a prophet is equated with obeying God, as Baqillani argues regarding Jesus, then how does your obedience to Prophet Muhammad translate into obedience to God? Are there areas where your actions contradict this unity of obedience?
?
Imagine someone uses a sacred text to prove a belief that you know is false. Would you be able to engage their text and offer a reinterpretation rather than simply rejecting it? What does that require of your own humility and understanding?

Alternative Interpretations of Jesus's Statement 'Before Abraham'
Baqillani refutes the Christian interpretation by offering alternative meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative interpretations Baqillani offers for Jesus's statement 'Before Abraham was, I am', and why they refute the Christian claim of divinity.

Core Idea
Jesus's words can be interpreted in multiple ways without implying pre-existence as God.

Baqillani begins by presenting the Christian argument: Jesus said 'I am before Abraham' while being a human descendant of Abraham, so his divinity must have existed before his humanity. Baqillani then offers three alternative interpretations that do not require divinity. This lesson examines those interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Christian argument
Read the first part where the Christian claim is presented: Jesus's statement proves his divinity because he speaks of being before Abraham.
2
Identify the three alternatives
Baqillani lists three possible meanings for Jesus's words. Find each one and note how each avoids implying divinity.
The alternatives are: religion known before Abraham, known in God's knowledge, or sent to the gathering before Abraham.
3
Understand the reason for rejection
Baqillani explains why affirming lordship for a body that ate and walked is impermissible, and why the union theory is far-fetched.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما وجبت الهية المسيح لأنه قال، وهو الصادق في قوله : «أنا قبل إبراهيم» وهو إنسان من ولد إبراهيم فعلمنا بذلك أنه قبل إبراهيم بلاهوته وابنه بناسوته . يقال لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون المراد بقوله: «أنا قبل إبراهيم» أن كثيراً من ديني وشرعي كان متعبداً به مشروعاً قبل إبراهيم على لسان بعض الرسل أو ما أنكرتم أن يكون أراد بقوله: «أنا قبل إبراهيم» أي مكتوب عند الله وأنا معروف قبل إبراهيم عند قوم من الملائكة أو أنا مبعوث إلى المحشر قبل إبراهيم إذ لا يجوز إثبات الربوبية بجسد أكل الطعام ومشي في الأسواق. والقول بأن اللاهوت اتحد به قول بعيد لا يحتمل التأويل وفي سفر الأمثال؛ وقد قال سليمان عليه السلام في كتابه: «أنا قبل الله سبحانه حيث مدَّ الأرض وكنت صبياً ألعب بين الدنيا وكنت مع يدي الله تعالى ولم يجب أن يكون سليمان قبل الدنيا أو الله سبحانه حيث مد الأرض بلاهوته، وأن يكون ابناً لدَاوُد بناسوته.
If they say: The divinity of the Messiah is necessarily established because he said, and he is truthful in his saying: 'I am before Abraham' while he is a human from the offspring of Abraham, so we know by that that he is before Abraham by his divinity and son by his humanity. It is said to them: Why do you not deny that the intended meaning of his saying 'I am before Abraham' is that much of my religion and law was worshiped and prescribed before Abraham through the tongue of some messengers? Or why do you not deny that he intended by his saying 'I am before Abraham' meaning written with God and I am known before Abraham among a group of angels, or I am sent to the assembly before Abraham? For it is not permissible to affirm lordship for a body that ate food and walked in the markets. And the saying that the divinity united with him is a far-fetched saying that does not permit interpretation. And in the Book of Proverbs, Solomon, peace be upon him, said in his book: 'I am before God Almighty when He spread the earth and I was a child playing between the two worlds and I was with the hands of God Almighty' and it was not necessary for Solomon to be before the world or God Almighty when He spread the earth by his divinity, and to be a son of David by his humanity.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian interpretation of Jesus's statement 'Before Abraham was, I am' as proof of pre-existence and divinity. Baqillani offers three alternative interpretations: that his religion was known before Abraham, that he was known in God's knowledge or among angels, or that he was sent to the assembly before Abraham. He then uses the analogy of Solomon's statement in Proverbs, which if taken literally would also imply pre-existence, but Christians do not consider Solomon divine. This shows inconsistency.

Key Terms
اللاهوت (al-lāhūt, divinity)
الناسوت (al-nāsūt, humanity)
المحشر (al-maḥshar, the gathering)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Christians think that Jesus's statement 'Before Abraham was, I am' is an explicit claim to be God.
Correction: Baqillani shows the statement can be interpreted in multiple ways that do not require divinity, such as referring to his religious law being earlier or his knowledge in God's plan.

Checkpoint
How many alternative interpretations does Baqillani offer for Jesus's statement 'I am before Abraham'?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that Jesus's words need not imply divinity by providing plausible alternative interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Bible or any scripture, do you tend to take the most literal meaning even when it leads to contradictions with clear truths? How can you guard against forcing your own preconceived interpretations onto the text?
?
Baqillani offers multiple plausible interpretations for Jesus's statement. In your own life, when faced with a difficult passage, do you rush to a single interpretation or do you patiently consider alternatives that might keep you from error? Commit to seeking multiple scholarly perspectives before settling on a meaning.
?
The Christian argument relies on a single phrase to affirm divinity. Are there areas in your own beliefs where you hold tenaciously to a proof text while ignoring the broader context or clear teachings? Ask Allah to grant you honesty with yourself and the humility to correct any misunderstandings.

The Solomon Analogy: Refuting Divinity from Pre-existence Claims
Baqillani uses the example of Solomon's saying to demonstrate inconsistency in Christian reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the analogy of Solomon's saying in the Book of Proverbs to refute the Christian argument for Jesus's divinity.

Core Idea
If Solomon's similar words don't prove his divinity, neither do Jesus's.

After offering alternative interpretations, Baqillani strengthens his refutation with a powerful analogy. He cites a passage from the Book of Proverbs where Solomon speaks in similar pre-existence language. Christians do not consider Solomon divine for such statements, so inconsistency reveals the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Baqillani quotes Solomon's words from Proverbs: 'I was before God when He spread the earth, I was a child playing...' Notice the language resembles Jesus's claim.
2
Apply the same logic
If Christians used the same reasoning for Solomon, they would have to claim he is divine. Since they don't, their argument for Jesus is inconsistent.
3
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the Christian claim is based on a faulty premise: linguistic similarity does not imply ontological reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما وجبت الهية المسيح لأنه قال، وهو الصادق في قوله : «أنا قبل إبراهيم» وهو إنسان من ولد إبراهيم فعلمنا بذلك أنه قبل إبراهيم بلاهوته وابنه بناسوته . يقال لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون المراد بقوله: «أنا قبل إبراهيم» أن كثيراً من ديني وشرعي كان متعبداً به مشروعاً قبل إبراهيم على لسان بعض الرسل أو ما أنكرتم أن يكون أراد بقوله: «أنا قبل إبراهيم» أي مكتوب عند الله وأنا معروف قبل إبراهيم عند قوم من الملائكة أو أنا مبعوث إلى المحشر قبل إبراهيم إذ لا يجوز إثبات الربوبية بجسد أكل الطعام ومشي في الأسواق. والقول بأن اللاهوت اتحد به قول بعيد لا يحتمل التأويل وفي سفر الأمثال؛ وقد قال سليمان عليه السلام في كتابه: «أنا قبل الله سبحانه حيث مدَّ الأرض وكنت صبياً ألعب بين الدنيا وكنت مع يدي الله تعالى ولم يجب أن يكون سليمان قبل الدنيا أو الله سبحانه حيث مد الأرض بلاهوته، وأن يكون ابناً لدَاوُد بناسوته.
If they say: The divinity of the Messiah is necessarily established because he said, and he is truthful in his saying: 'I am before Abraham' while he is a human from the offspring of Abraham, so we know by that that he is before Abraham by his divinity and son by his humanity. It is said to them: Why do you not deny that the intended meaning of his saying 'I am before Abraham' is that much of my religion and law was worshiped and prescribed before Abraham through the tongue of some messengers? Or why do you not deny that he intended by his saying 'I am before Abraham' meaning written with God and I am known before Abraham among a group of angels, or I am sent to the assembly before Abraham? For it is not permissible to affirm lordship for a body that ate food and walked in the markets. And the saying that the divinity united with him is a far-fetched saying that does not permit interpretation. And in the Book of Proverbs, Solomon, peace be upon him, said in his book: 'I am before God Almighty when He spread the earth and I was a child playing between the two worlds and I was with the hands of God Almighty' and it was not necessary for Solomon to be before the world or God Almighty when He spread the earth by his divinity, and to be a son of David by his humanity.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian interpretation of Jesus's statement 'Before Abraham was, I am' as proof of pre-existence and divinity. Baqillani offers three alternative interpretations: that his religion was known before Abraham, that he was known in God's knowledge or among angels, or that he was sent to the assembly before Abraham. He then uses the analogy of Solomon's statement in Proverbs, which if taken literally would also imply pre-existence, but Christians do not consider Solomon divine. This shows inconsistency.

Key Terms
اللاهوت (al-lāhūt, divinity)
الناسوت (al-nāsūt, humanity)
سفر الأمثال (Sifr al-Amthāl, Book of Proverbs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any claim of pre-existence in scripture must indicate divinity.
Correction: Baqillani shows that Solomon made similar claims without being divine, so pre-existence language is not exclusive to God.

Checkpoint
Which prophet does Baqillani use in his analogy from the Book of Proverbs?
A) Moses
B) Solomon
C) David
D) Abraham

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's analogy with Solomon demonstrates that pre-existence language does not automatically prove divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious argument simply because it sounds profound or comes from a sacred text, without checking its consistency with other clear truths? Where in your own beliefs might you be guilty of that?
?
This lesson shows how scholars use rational arguments to defend the truth. What rational argument for your faith can you articulate today that goes beyond mere emotion or tradition? Are you able to defend it when challenged?
?
The Christian claim about Jesus is refuted by an analogy with Solomon. Think of a false belief about God that you once held or hear others hold. What would it take for you to apply the same rigorous reasoning to reject that falsehood, even if it is comforting?

Brahmin Classification and First Argument
Baqillani presents the Brahmin classification and their first rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of the Brahmins, the correction of their name, and their first rational argument against prophethood.

Core Idea
The Brahmins deny prophethood by claiming that anything rational needs no messenger.

Imam Baqillani begins his discussion with the Brahmins by clarifying their identity and their two opinions. He corrects a common misconception about their name and then presents their first argument, which uses a dichotomy of rational versus non-rational to render prophethood unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Notice that the Brahmins split into two opinions, one of which denies messengers entirely. The author also corrects the mistaken attribution of their name to Ibrahim.
The Brahmins are actually named after a man called Brahma.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument claims that whatever a messenger brings must be either rational or not. If rational, reason suffices; if not rational, it is unacceptable. Consider how this argument tries to eliminate the need for prophets.
This argument is a foundational pillar of their rejection.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Ask yourself: Is the dichotomy exhaustive? Could there be things that are both rational and require a messenger? This will prepare you for Baqillani's refutation later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على البراهمة وقد افترقت البراهمة على قولين : فمنهم من جحد الرسل، وزعم أنه لا يجوز في حكمة الله هم من الناس يظن أنهم سموا براهمة لانتسابهم إلى إبراهيم عليه السلام وذلك خطأ، فإن هؤلاء المخصوصون بنفي النبوات أصلا ورأساً فكيف يقولون بإبراهيم عليه السلام والقوم الذين اعتقدوا نبوة إبراهيم عليه السلام من أهل الهند فهم الثنوية منهم القائلون بالنور والظلمة على رأي أصحاب الاثنين وهؤلاء البراهمة إنما انتسبوا إلى رجل منهم يقال له براهم، وقد مهد لهم نفي النبوات أصلا وقرر استحالة ذلك في عقولهم بوجوه . منها أن قال: إن الذي يأتي به الرسول لم يخل من أحد أمرين : إما أن يكون معقولاً وإما أن لا يكون معقولاً. فإن كان معقولاً فقد كفانا العقل التام بإدراكه والوصول إليه، فأي حاجة لنا إلى الرسول؟ وإن لم يكن معقولاً فلا يكون مقبولاً ؛ إذ قبول ما ليس بمعقول خروج عن حد الإنسانية، ودخول في حريم البهيمية . ومنها أن قال : قد دل العقل على أن الله تعالى حكيم، والحكيم لا يتعبد الخلق إلا بما تدل عليه عقولهم. وقد دلت الدلائل العقلية على أن للعالم صانعاً قادراً حكيماً وأنه أنعم على عباده نعماً توجب الشكر، فننظر في آيات خلقه بعقولنا ونشكره بآلائه علينا. وإذا عرفناه وشكرنا لـه استوجبنا ثوابه. وإذا أنكرناه وكفرنا به استوجبنا عقابه فما بالنا نتبع بشراً مثلنا؟ فإنه إن كان يأمرنا بما يخالف ذلك كان قوله دليلاً ظاهراً على كذبه . ومنها أن قال : قد دلّ العقل على أن للعالم صانعاً حكيماً ، والحكيم لا يتعبد الخلق بما يقبح في عقولهم وقد ردت أصحاب الشرائع بمستقبحات من حيث العقل، من التوجه إلى البيت مخصوص في العبادة والطواف حوله والسعي ورمي الحجار والإحرام، والتلبية، وتقبيل الحجر الأصم وكذلك ذبح الحيوان وتحريم ما يمكن أن يكون غذاء الإنسان وتحليل ما ينقص من بنيته، وغير ذلك، وكل هذه الأمور مخالفة لقضايا العقول . ومنها أنه قال : إن أكبر الكبائر في الرسالة اتباع رجل هو مثلك في الصورة والنفس والعقل يأكل مما تأكل ويشرب مما تشرب حتى تكون بالنسبة إليه كجماد يتصرف فيك رفعاً ووضاعاً أو كحيوان يصرفك أماماً وخلقاً أو كعبد يتقدم إليك أمراً ونهياً فأي تميز له عليك؟ وأية فضيلة أوجبت استخدامك؟ وما دليله على صدق دعواه ؟ فإن اغترر ثم بمجرد قوله فلا تمييز لقول على قول وإن انحسرتم بحجته ومعجزته فعندنا من خصائص الجواهر والأجسام ما لا يحصى كثيره .
Chapter on the discussion with the Brahmins. And the Brahmins have split into two opinions: Among them are those who denied the messengers and claimed that it is not permissible in the wisdom of Allah. They are among the people who think that they were named Brahmins due to their affiliation to Ibrahim, peace be upon him, and that is a mistake, for these are specifically those who deny prophethood absolutely and fundamentally, so how could they affirm Ibrahim, peace be upon him? And the people who believed in the prophethood of Ibrahim, peace be upon him, from the people of India are the Dualists, among them those who say light and darkness according to the opinion of the proponents of dualism. And these Brahmins are only affiliated to a man among them called Brahma, and he laid for them the absolute denial of prophethood and established its impossibility in their minds with several aspects. Among them: He said: That which the messenger brings is not free from one of two matters: either it is rational (ma'qul) or it is not rational. If it is rational, then perfect reason sufficed us to perceive it and attain it, so what need do we have for the messenger? And if it is not rational, then it is not acceptable, for accepting what is not rational is an exit from the limit of humanity and an entry into the domain of bestiality. And among them: He said: Reason has indicated that Allah Almighty is wise, and the wise does not obligate creation except with what their minds indicate. And rational proofs have indicated that the world has a powerful, wise Maker and that He has bestowed upon His servants blessings that necessitate gratitude, so we look at the signs of His creation with our minds and thank Him for His favors upon us. And when we know Him and thank Him, we deserve His reward. And when we deny Him and disbelieve in Him, we deserve His punishment. So why should we follow a human like us? For if he commands us with what contradicts that, then his statement is a clear proof of his lying. And among them: He said: Reason has indicated that the world has a wise Maker, and the wise does not obligate creation with what is repugnant in their minds. And the adherents of the revealed laws have come with things that are repugnant from the perspective of reason, such as facing a particular house in worship, circumambulating it, running, throwing stones, entering ihram, talbiya, kissing the black stone, likewise slaughtering animals, prohibiting what can be human food, and permitting what diminishes the body, and other things. And all these matters contradict the rulings of intellects. And among them: He said: The greatest of major sins regarding the message is following a man who is like you in form, soul, and mind, eating what you eat and drinking what you drink, until you become in relation to him like an inanimate object he disposes of raising and lowering, or like an animal he directs forward and backward, or like a slave who commands and forbids you. So what distinction does he have over you? And what virtue obliged your subjugation? And what is his proof of the truth of his claim? For if you are deceived by his mere statement, then there is no distinction of one statement over another. And if you are overwhelmed by his proof and miracle, then we have from the properties of substances and bodies an incalculable number.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the Brahmins' position and arguments against prophethood. It begins with a classification: the Brahmins split into two opinions, one of which denies messengers entirely. The author then digresses to correct a common error about their name—they are not named after Ibrahim but after a man called Brahma. Brahma laid the foundation for denying prophethood by establishing rational proofs of impossibility.

The author then lists four arguments (وجوه) from the Brahmins:
1. The dichotomy argument: Whatever a messenger brings is either rational or not. If rational, reason is sufficient; if not rational, it is unacceptable as it dehumanizes.
2. The argument from divine wisdom: God is wise and only commands what reason dictates. Reason already teaches gratitude and knowledge of God, so prophets are redundant. Any command contrary to reason shows the prophet is lying.
3. The argument from repugnant practices: Revealed religions mandate actions that are repugnant to reason (facing a house, circumambulation, sacrifice, etc.). A wise God would not command such things.
4. The argument from human equality: A prophet is a human like us with no inherent superiority. Following such a person is a great sin. There is no proof except his own claim, and if miracles are cited, there are countless natural wonders that could be claimed as such.

Each argument is presented in the Brahmins' own voice, with the author not yet offering a refutation in this passage.

Key Terms
براهمة
معقول
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Brahmins are named after Ibrahim.
Correction: The text explains they are named after a man called Brahma, not Ibrahim.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what is the status of something that is not rational?
A) It must be accepted on faith.
B) It is unacceptable and dehumanizing.
C) It is a miracle.
D) It is subject to interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The Brahmins' first argument attempts to prove that prophethood is either unnecessary (if rational) or impossible (if irrational).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied solely on your own reasoning to decide a matter of faith, ignoring the guidance of prophets? How does the Brahmin argument, which claims reason is enough, expose a hidden pride in your heart, and what will you do today to submit your intellect to revelation?
?
When you encounter an Islamic teaching that seems 'irrational' to your mind, do you feel tempted to dismiss it or reinterpret it? How does this lesson's warning about accepting only the rational challenge you to trust in Allah's wisdom even when you do not fully understand?
?
The Brahmins' leader established a foundation for denying prophethood. Is there any influence—from your environment, culture, or your own desires—that subtly leads you to question the necessity of the Prophet ﷺ? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen your reliance on his guidance?

Second Argument: Reason and Divine Wisdom
Baqillani presents the Brahmin argument that reason and divine wisdom make prophethood redundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmin argument that reason alone suffices to know God and render prophets unnecessary.

Core Idea
If God is wise, He would only command what reason dictates, making prophets redundant.

The second Brahmin argument builds on the concept of divine wisdom: since God is wise, He would not burden humans with commands beyond their reason. Reason already leads to knowledge of God and gratitude, so prophets are unnecessary. Any prophetic command contrary to reason is a sign of falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Trace the logical flow
The argument proceeds: Reason shows God is wise. A wise being commands only what reason endorses. Reason already tells us to know and thank God. Therefore, prophets add nothing new.
2
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that following a human prophet is pointless; if a prophet commands something contrary to reason, that itself proves him a liar.
3
Consider the underlying assumption
The argument assumes that reason fully suffices for knowing God and His will. Do you agree? This is a key point for refutation.

According to the second argument, what is the proof that a prophet is lying?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that your reason struggles to justify, do you submit like a servant or do you internally echo the Brahmin's skepticism? Which area of your life reveals that your reason still sits on the throne?
?
You claim to believe that God is wise—but do you live as though His wisdom only includes what you can understand? What prophetic teaching have you neglected because it didn't 'make sense' to you, and what would it cost you to obey it today?
?
The Brahmin argued that knowing and thanking God is enough. Yet, how often do your moments of gratitude actually flow from prophetic guidance rather than just your own reflection? Name one specific sunnah that has shaped your thankfulness this week—if you cannot, what does that say about your need for prophets?

Third Argument: Repugnant Religious Practices
Baqillani presents the Brahmin argument that religious practices are repugnant to reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmin argument that revealed religions command practices that are repugnant to reason, which a wise God would not do.

Core Idea
Since God is wise, He would not command what reason finds repugnant, yet religions do.

The third argument shifts to specific religious practices that the Brahmins consider irrational: facing a house, circumambulation, running, throwing stones, and others. They claim that a wise God would never impose such actions because they are repugnant to reason. This is an attempt to show that revealed laws cannot be from God.

Guided Path
1
List the practices mentioned
Identify each practice: facing a house, circumambulation, running, throwing stones, ihram, talbiya, kissing the black stone, slaughtering animals, prohibitions, etc.
These are specific Islamic rituals.
2
Understand the argument's core
The Brahmins argue that a wise God would not command what reason deems repugnant. Since these practices appear irrational, the laws cannot be divine.
The argument hinges on the assumption that reason universally repudiates these acts.
3
Reflect on the assumption
Consider: Are these practices truly irrational? Could there be wisdom beyond apparent repugnance? This is a crucial point for refutation.

لقضايا العقول . ومنها أنه قال : إن أكبر الكبائر في الرسالة اتباع رجل هو مثلك في الصورة والنفس والعقل يأكل مما تأكل ويشرب مما تشرب حتى تكون بالنسبة إليه كجماد يتصرف فيك رفعاً ووضاعاً أو كحيوان يصرفك أماماً وخلقاً أو كعبد يتقدم إليك أمراً ونهياً فأي تميز له عليك؟ وأية فضيلة أوجبت استخدامك؟ وما دليله على صدق دعواه ؟ فإن اغترر ثم بمجرد قوله فلا تمييز لقول على قول وإن انحسرتم بحجته ومعجزته فعندنا من خصائص الجواهر والأجسام ما لا يحصى كثيره .
Chapter on the discussion with the Brahmins. And the Brahmins have split into two opinions: Among them are those who denied the messengers and claimed that it is not permissible in the wisdom of Allah. They are among the people who think that they were named Brahmins due to their affiliation to Ibrahim, peace be upon him, and that is a mistake, for these are specifically those who deny prophethood absolutely and fundamentally, so how could they affirm Ibrahim, peace be upon him? And the people who believed in the prophethood of Ibrahim, peace be upon him, from the people of India are the Dualists, among them those who say light and darkness according to the opinion of the proponents of dualism. And these Brahmins are only affiliated to a man among them called Brahma, and he laid for them the absolute denial of prophethood and established its impossibility in their minds with several aspects. Among them: He said: That which the messenger brings is not free from one of two matters: either it is rational (ma'qul) or it is not rational. If it is rational, then perfect reason sufficed us to perceive it and attain it, so what need do we have for the messenger? And if it is not rational, then it is not acceptable, for accepting what is not rational is an exit from the limit of humanity and an entry into the domain of bestiality. And among them: He said: Reason has indicated that Allah Almighty is wise, and the wise does not obligate creation except with what their minds indicate. And rational proofs have indicated that the world has a powerful, wise Maker and that He has bestowed upon His servants blessings that necessitate gratitude, so we look at the signs of His creation with our minds and thank Him for His favors upon us. And when we know Him and thank Him, we deserve His reward. And when we deny Him and disbelieve in Him, we deserve His punishment. So why should we follow a human like us? For if he commands us with what contradicts that, then his statement is a clear proof of his lying. And among them: He said: Reason has indicated that the world has a wise Maker, and the wise does not obligate creation with what is repugnant in their minds. And the adherents of the revealed laws have come with things that are repugnant from the perspective of reason, such as facing a particular house in worship, circumambulating it, running, throwing stones, entering ihram, talbiya, kissing the black stone, likewise slaughtering animals, prohibiting what can be human food, and permitting what diminishes the body, and other things. And all these matters contradict the rulings of intellects. And among them: He said: The greatest of major sins regarding the message is following a man who is like you in form, soul, and mind, eating what you eat and drinking what you drink, until you become in relation to him like an inanimate object he disposes of raising and lowering, or like an animal he directs forward and backward, or like a slave who commands and forbids you. So what distinction does he have over you? And what virtue obliged your subjugation? And what is his proof of the truth of his claim? For if you are deceived by his mere statement, then there is no distinction of one statement over another. And if you are overwhelmed by his proof and miracle, then we have from the properties of substances and bodies an incalculable number.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the Brahmins' position and arguments against prophethood. It begins with a classification: the Brahmins split into two opinions, one of which denies messengers entirely. The author then digresses to correct a common error about their name—they are not named after Ibrahim but after a man called Brahma. Brahma laid the foundation for denying prophethood by establishing rational proofs of impossibility.

The author then lists four arguments (وجوه) from the Brahmins:
1. The dichotomy argument: Whatever a messenger brings is either rational or not. If rational, reason is sufficient; if not rational, it is unacceptable as it dehumanizes.
2. The argument from divine wisdom: God is wise and only commands what reason dictates. Reason already teaches gratitude and knowledge of God, so prophets are redundant. Any command contrary to reason shows the prophet is lying.
3. The argument from repugnant practices: Revealed religions mandate actions that are repugnant to reason (facing a house, circumambulation, sacrifice, etc.). A wise God would not command such things.
4. The argument from human equality: A prophet is a human like us with no inherent superiority. Following such a person is a great sin. There is no proof except his own claim, and if miracles are cited, there are countless natural wonders that could be claimed as such.

Each argument is presented in the Brahmins' own voice, with the author not yet offering a refutation in this passage.

Key Terms
مستقبحات
العقل
شرائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all rational people find these practices repugnant.
Correction: The argument assumes universal rational repugnance, but many find wisdom in these rites.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why do the Brahmins reject Islamic practices?
A) They are too difficult.
B) They are repugnant to reason.
C) They are not mentioned in the Vedas.
D) They require faith.

Key Takeaway
The third argument attempts to discredit revealed laws by claiming their practices are rationally repugnant, implying a wise God would not command them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever questioned the wisdom of an Islamic ruling because it seemed unreasonable to you? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to Allah's commands even when you don't see the wisdom?
?
The Brahmins used reason to reject divine revelation. In what areas of your life do you rely more on your own reasoning than on what Allah has commanded? What would it cost you to surrender that area completely?
?
Think of a specific Islamic practice you find difficult or uncomfortable. Is your discomfort rooted in genuine rational objection or in personal desire? Are you willing to obey regardless of your feelings?

Fourth Argument: Human Equality and Lack of Proof
Baqillani presents the Brahmin argument about human equality and the insufficiency of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Brahmin argument that a prophet has no inherent superiority and that miracles do not prove prophethood.

Core Idea
A prophet is just a human like you, so following him is a major sin; miracles are not convincing.

The fourth and most provocative argument asserts that obeying a prophet is the greatest sin because he is your equal in humanity. The Brahmins challenge any claim of distinction: no virtue justifies subjugation, and mere words prove nothing. Even miracles are dismissed, as there are countless unexplained phenomena in nature.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim of equality
The Brahmins argue that a prophet is identical in form, soul, and mind, eating and drinking like any human. Therefore, following him is like being an inanimate object or an animal controlled by another.
2
Analyze the dismissal of proof
The argument says: if you accept his mere statement, any liar can claim the same. If you rely on his miracle, there are countless natural wonders that could be claimed as miracles.
This challenges the uniqueness of prophetic miracles.
3
Reflect on the underlying assumption
The Brahmins assume that physical humanity precludes any spiritual authority. Is this valid?

المخصوصون بنفي النبوات أصلا ورأساً فكيف يقولون بإبراهيم عليه السلام والقوم الذين اعتقدوا نبوة إبراهيم عليه السلام من أهل الهند فهم الثنوية منهم القائلون بالنور والظلمة على رأي أصحاب الاثنين وهؤلاء The Two Opinions of the Barāhima
Classification of the Barāhima's two opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions of the Barāhima regarding prophethood and their reasoning.

Core Idea
The Barāhima split into two groups: one denies all messengers, the other limits prophethood to Adam or Abraham.

This passage from the Tamhid al-Awaa'il presents the doctrine of the Barāhima, a sect known for their rejection of prophethood. Imam Baqillani begins by outlining their two principal opinions, which form the basis for the subsequent theological refutation. Understanding these positions is crucial for grasping the classical Sunni argument for the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'افترقت البراهمة على قولين' which sets the structure.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage presents two groups of Barāhima. Find each one and notice how the author describes their position.
The first group denies all messengers; the second accepts only some.
3
Understand the reasoning of the first group
The first group claims that sending a messenger is not permissible in God's wisdom. Ask yourself why they might think that.
This will be refuted later in the text.
4
Note the subdivisions in the second group
The second group splits further: some accept only Adam, others only Abraham. This shows internal disagreement.
The passage calls this the 'summary of their statement'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على البراهمة. وقد افترقت البراهمة على قولين: فمنهم من جحد الرسل، وزعم أنه لا يجوز في حكمة الله سبحانه وصفته أن يبعث رسولاً إلى خلقه، وأنه لا وجه من ناحيته الرسالة عن الخالق سبحانه. وقال الفريق الآخر: إن الله تعالى ما أرسل رسولاً سوى آدم عليه السلام وكذبوا كل مدع للنبوة سواه. وقال قوم منهم: بل ما بعث الله تعالى غير إبراهيم وحده وأنكروا نبوة من سواه. وهذا جملة قولهم.
Chapter: Discourse on the Barāhima. And the Barāhima split into two opinions: among them are those who denied the messengers and claimed that it is not permissible in the wisdom of God Most High and His attribute that He send a messenger to His creation, and that there is no aspect from His side for receiving the message from the Creator Most High. And the other group said: God Most High did not send a messenger except Adam, peace be upon him, and they denied every claimant to prophethood besides him. And some of them said: Rather, God sent none other than Abraham alone, and they denied the prophethood of others besides him. This is the summary of their statement.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage classifies the Barāhima into two main groups. The author begins by stating that the Barāhima divided into two opinions. The first group completely denies all messengers, claiming that it is not consistent with God's wisdom and attributes to send a messenger to His creation, and that there is no way for a messenger to receive revelation from the Creator. The second group, however, does not deny prophethood entirely but limits it to specific prophets: some say only Adam was sent, while others say only Abraham was sent. The passage ends by stating that this is the summary of their statements, setting the stage for the detailed arguments that follow.

Key Terms
براهمة (Barāhima)
رسل (messengers)
حكمة الله (wisdom of God)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Barāhima completely deny God or reject all divine revelation.
Correction: The passage shows they only deny or limit prophethood; they do not necessarily deny God's existence or wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did the Barāhima split?
A) Into three groups
B) Into two groups: those who deny all messengers and those who accept only Adam or Abraham
C) Into two groups: those who accept all prophets and those who reject all
D) Into one group

Key Takeaway
The Barāhima held two main positions on prophethood: absolute denial or acceptance limited to a single prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that some Barāhima claimed 'there is no aspect from His side for receiving the message,' do you see any trace of that same arrogance in your own heart? In what ways have you felt that God should communicate in a way that makes sense to you, rather than humbly submitting to His chosen method of revelation?
?
The Barāhima limited prophethood to one or two individuals. Are there areas in your life where you have limited God's guidance to only what is comfortable or familiar, ignoring other clear messengers or commands? What would it look like for you to fully accept all that God has sent, even the parts that challenge your preferences?
?
Have you ever found yourself doubting the necessity of a particular prophet or message because it doesn't fit your own understanding of God? How does the Barāhima's rejection of all messengers except Adam or Abraham challenge you to examine your own assumptions about God's wisdom and His choice of messengers?

The Three Sects of the Barāhima
The author enumerates three sects of the Barāhima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Barāhima were not a monolithic group but subdivided into at least three distinct sects with different beliefs.

Core Idea
The Barāhima split into sects with different beliefs.

This lesson shows that after the author's general refutation, the Barāhima are further distinguished into separate groups. The passage lists three sects, indicating diversity within the broader category. Pay attention to the names as they hint at their distinctive doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise style — the author simply lists the three sects.
2
Identify the three sects
Find the three terms: aṣḥāb al-badada, aṣḥāb al-fikra, and aṣḥāb al-tanāsukh. Consider what each name might imply about their beliefs.
Badada likely refers to idol worship or Buddha; fikra means 'thought'; tanāsukh is reincarnation.
3
Reflect on the diversity
Think about why the author mentions these divisions. It shows that not all Barāhima were atheists; they held varied views, including reincarnation.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that all Barāhima were atheists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن البراهمة تفرقوا أصنافاً، فمنهم أصحاب البددة ومنهم أصحاب الفكرة ومنهم أصحاب التناسخ.
Then indeed the Barāhima split into categories: among them are the people of buddha, and among them are the people of thought, and among them are the people of metempsychosis.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This short passage notes that after the previous discussion, the Barāhima further subdivided into various sects. The author lists three: 'aṣḥāb al-badada' (possibly worshipers of Buddha or idols), 'aṣḥāb al-fikra' (those who rely on intellectual speculation), and 'aṣḥāb al-tanāsukh' (believers in reincarnation or metempsychosis). This indicates that the Barāhima are not all uniform in their doctrines.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Barāhima were atheists who denied any spiritual belief.
Correction: The text shows that the Barāhima included sects like those believing in reincarnation (tanāsukh) and idol worship (badada), so they held diverse spiritual views.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Barāhima divided into sects with conflicting beliefs. Are you aware of the subtle ways your own beliefs might be influenced by cultural assumptions rather than revelation? What would it take for you to sincerely examine your own foundational beliefs?
?
This passage shows that people can hold contradictory beliefs even within a single tradition. Have you ever uncritically adopted a belief without verifying it against the Qur'an and Sunnah? What is one belief you hold that you have never deeply questioned?
?
The Barāhima had sects that relied on idol worship, intellect alone, or reincarnation. Which of these tendencies do you find most appealing to the human soul? How can you guard against your own inclinations leading you away from the truth?
